The ultimate intent in the creation and formation of the worlds is that the Holy One, Blessed be He, desired to have a dwelling place in the lower worlds, meaning in this physical and material world, than which there is nothing lower. As it is written in Tanya, it is impossible to say that the intent is in the upper worlds, since for them it is [only] a descent from the light of His countenance. And like the world of Atzilus—it is impossible to say that the intent is in the world of Atzilus, for Atzilus is only the revelation of the concealment, and as it is in its concealment, the light is loftier, for revelations are diminished in their drawing down below. It is impossible to say that one should "utilize" a loftier matter for a matter lower than it. Rather, the intent is in the worlds of Beriah, Yetzirah, and Asiyah, and in Beriah, Yetzirah, and Asiyah themselves, in this physical and material world, for this descent is a necessity for an ascent, for "when the Sitra Achra is subdued, the glory of the Holy One, Blessed be He, is exalted in all the worlds," that such a matter is drawn down that is in all the worlds equally—namely, the Essence of the Ein Sof, Blessed be He. For the revelations are diminished in their drawing down, as mentioned above, and if so, they are not in all the worlds equally; specifically the Essence is in all worlds equally, and the drawing down of the Essence is "when the Sitra Achra is subdued," meaning in this lower world.

And in this world itself, there are four types: inanimate, vegetative, animate, and speaking. Since the intent is to have a dwelling place in the lower worlds, the ultimate intent is specifically in the inanimate type. Therefore, the ultimate intent is specifically in the descent of the soul into the body, for the manner of the creation of the human body is changed from the bodies of other creatures, for they were all created in their form. If so, there is a relationship and connection between their body and their soul, unlike in man, of whom it is written, "Your eyes saw my unformed substance," and our Sages said that at first He formed him as a golem, an inanimate aspect, and only afterwards breathed a soul into him, because specifically in the inanimate, the intent of a "dwelling place in the lower worlds"—the drawing down of the Essence—is completed.

This drawing down is through the souls, because the souls are not separate, as the saying goes, "The souls of Israel arose in thought," and in thought itself they are on the highest level within it—this is the precision of the language "arose in thought"—to the point that they are rooted in the Essence, for in the knowledge of Himself, He knows the souls. Because of this, that in the knowledge of Himself He knows the souls, then wherever they are, in the entire order of the chain of progression, and also in the human body, the matter of His knowledge of Himself—that He and His knowledge are one—is felt in them, and they draw down the Essence. This drawing down is through the Torah, because the Torah also has its root in the Essence. When souls and Torah come together, they draw down the Essence into the human body, the aspect of the inanimate. Therefore, this drawing down is in the aspect of "these are the things," specifically the "work of the weekday," the aspect of the inanimate, for specifically in this is the intent of a "dwelling place in the lower worlds" completed. This is "These are the things that Hashem has commanded to do them"—"commanded" (tzivah) from the language of "connection" (tzavta) and union, for through physical things, one connects and unites with the Essence.

7) It is known that the foundation of the entire order of the chain of progression is on the three matters of "world, year, soul." This is what is explained here: these three matters. At first, he explains the matter of the soul: "Moshe assembled the entire congregation of the Children of Israel," that he assembled all the souls of Israel to the aspect of true unity. This is done specifically through the power of Moshe, because Moshe's root is from the first Shemitah, meaning above the chain of progression, above our Shemitah. Afterwards, he continues, "And said to them, 'These are the things...'"—the aspect of "work of the weekday," the aspect of "world." For the soul itself does not need rectification, and the intent of its descent below is only to refine the body, the animal soul, and its portion in the world. And since the connection of the soul and the world is through "year," the aspect of time, therefore Moshe explained the order of avodah in the framework of time, which is his saying, "Six days shall work be done, and on the seventh day, Shabbos Shabbaton."

Just as this matter is in the entirety of all Israel, so it is also individually in each and every one of Israel, that he must occupy himself with his avodah in these three matters. The matter of "Moshe assembled the entire congregation of the Children of Israel" is that he must gather and assemble all his scattered matters, and this matter is through the power of Moshe that is in each and every one of Israel, as explained in Tanya according to the saying of our Sages, "For Moshe, it is a small thing," which refers to the aspect of Moshe that is in each and every one of Israel. The matter of "These are the things that Hashem has commanded to do them" refers to the occupation with the "work of the weekday" in the manner of "commanded"—from the language of connection and union—meaning to connect his physical matters with Elokus, and this is the ultimate intent of the descent of the soul into the body. All this is through the avodah in time, "Six days shall work be done," and through this one comes to the aspect of Shabbos Shabbaton. And he juxtaposed Shabbos to the work of the Mishkan, for through the aforementioned avodah, the construction of the Third Beis HaMikdash will be, speedily in our days.

## Sicha of Shabbos Parshas Vayakhel-Pekudei, Parshas HaChodesh, Mevarchim HaChodesh Nissan, 5712.

**Unedited**

**A.** The Rebbe instructed that a *nigun* be played and then recited a *maamar* beginning with the words "Vayakhel Moshe..."

**B.** In *Parshas HaChodesh*, the mitzvah of eating *matzah* is also stated: "For seven days you shall eat *matzos*".
By way of introduction, this mitzvah is unique in that it remains a Torah obligation even in the present time.
Generally speaking, there are three primary elements of the holiday of Pesach: *Pesach*, *matzah*, and *maror*. In the present time, the fulfillment of the mitzvah of *Pesach* is only through "and we will render [the offering of] our lips [in place of] bulls". The mitzvah of eating *maror* is only by Rabbinic decree, whereas the mitzvah of eating *matzah* is a Torah obligation.

**C.** The difference between *chametz* and *matzah* lies in two matters:
(1) With *chametz*, the dough rises and expands, whereas with *matzah*, the dough remains as it is. The spiritual significance of this is: *chametz* represents *yeshus* (ego) and arrogance; *matzah* represents *bitul* (self-nullification).
For this reason, the *essence* of *matzah* is specifically "bread of affliction" (*lechem oni*) [which is why, even though it is permitted to eat "enriched *matzah*" on Pesach, one cannot fulfill the mitzvah of eating *matzah* except with "bread of affliction"] —because the nature of *matzah* is *bitul* (as mentioned above), and this is expressed more fully in "bread of affliction". It should be added that the *matzah* the Jewish people ate in Egypt was also "bread of affliction".

(2) The words *matzah* and *chametz* are composed of identical letters, except that *matzah* is spelled with the letter *hei* and *chametz* is spelled with the letter *ches*.
The significance of this is:
The letter *hei* resembles the letter *ches*; both are composed of three lines and are open at the bottom. The difference between them is that the letter *ches* is completely closed on all three sides, whereas the letter *hei* has an opening at the top.
The opening at the bottom (which exists in both letters) signifies: "sin crouches at the entrance". The closure on all sides in the letter *ches* signifies that there is no room to "exit" from the state of "sin crouches at the entrance." The opening at the top in the letter *hei* signifies that although a person may find themselves in a state where "sin crouches at the entrance," nevertheless, they have an opening *above*—through which they can "exit" their state and perform *teshuvah*.
And even though the opening at the top (in the letter *hei*) is extremely small, our Sages have said : "Open for Me [an opening] like the eye of a needle, and I will open for you [an opening] like the entrance of a hall." For through a single *hirhur teshuvah* (thought of repentance), a person can change—in a single moment—from a completely wicked person to a completely righteous person.

**D.** The two aforementioned differences between *chametz* and *matzah* are interdependent:
When a person is in a state of *bitul* (which is the concept of *matzah*), then even if they sin, G-d forbid, they think about *performing teshuvah* (which is the concept of the letter *hei*). But when a person is in a state of *yeshus* and arrogance (which is the concept of *chametz*), they *do not* think about performing *teshuvah* (corresponding to the nature of the letter *ches*).
The reason for this is that when a person is *batel*, they do not strive to justify themselves; consequently, they make an honest accounting of all their deeds, and when they realize that their conduct is not as it should be, they perform *teshuvah*.
In contrast, as a result of the feeling of pride and arrogance, a person finds justifications to *defend* all of their conduct.

**E.** Furthermore:
Not only does the person find an excuse after committing an undesirable act (because they want to remain righteous in their own eyes), but even *ab initio*, when they arrive at the fulfillment of a mitzvah for which they must exert self-restraint, they find calculations and reasons to avoid fulfilling it because of their pride and arrogance.
An example of this is the mitzvah of *tzedakah*. Since *tzedakah* is the totality of all the mitzvos, one can understand from it the attitude toward all mitzvos:
When they see a poor person, they raise the argument that "Turnusrufus the wicked asked Rabbi Akiva: 'If your G-d loves the poor, why does He not provide for them?'".
There are two details in this argument—regarding oneself and regarding the other:
Because of their pride and arrogance, they calculate that all the wealth in their possession came to them because they *deserve* it, and indeed, they deserve *more* than that. Since that is the case, they argue, how can one give it to others?
And because their own *yeshus* does not "allow" for the existence of the other, it seems perfectly logical to them that the other is not like them, and they conclude that the reason the other is poor is because they *do not* deserve sustenance. Consequently, they think: Since *Hashem* wants—as justice indeed demands—that this person be poor, should *I* act in opposition to that and give them money?
Conversely, when one is *batel*:
(1) One makes an accounting and judges *oneself* as to whether one is truly better than the other. This accounting leads one to give *tzedakah*, as it is written : "You have established justice and *tzedakah* in Yaakov." The "*mishpat*" (judgment) that a person makes upon themselves leads them to "*tzedakah*."
(2) One understands that even if the lack of sustenance for the other is due to a punishment, G-d forbid, the fact that *they* have sustenance—regardless of their own status or undesirable state—compels them to say that the Jewish people are like *children* to Hashem. When a king punishes his son and a friend of the king grants him a favor, the king himself is very pleased by this, as in the response of Rabbi Akiva (to the argument of Turnusrufus mentioned above).
And just as it is with the mitzvah of *tzedakah*, so it is with all the mitzvos.

**F.** In addition to the fact that because of their pride and arrogance they seek a calculation to prove that their conduct is correct, even when they are unable to find any excuse for their actions and are forced to admit that they are not behaving as required, they still find *various reasons* that led them to this, and thus they remain *righteous* in their own eyes!
They raise various arguments:
Regarding one thing, they argue that it is due to the *yetzer hara*, for "its inclination burns like fire". They argue that the Gemara itself says that Hashem regrets having created the *yetzer hara*; it is therefore impossible to demand of them why they did not defeat the *yetzer hara*! Regarding another thing, they argue that "their place caused them to sin", and so on.
They use all the calculations mentioned in *Tanya* —which are intended to be used to judge *others* favorably—for *themselves*. Moreover, they think that in light of the circumstances in which they find themselves, "it is actually quite good that they are as they are." Someone else in their place "would have been who knows what!"
Furthermore:
Even when they know that their conduct is *not good*, and they have no *reasons* to attribute it to, nevertheless, "love covers all transgressions".
The precision of the word "transgressions" (*pesha'im*) is as our Sages say : "These are acts of rebellion." That is to say, even when they have no excuse to justify their actions, as they themselves know they are *rebelling* (G-d forbid), nevertheless, "love covers all transgressions." They cannot negate the transgressions, even with false arguments, so they *cover* them with *self-love*.

**G.** This is the difference between *chametz* and *matzah*:
*Chametz* signifies self-aggrandizement, and as a result, *chametz* is composed of the letter *ches*, which is open at the bottom—"sin crouches at the entrance"—and is closed on all other sides, meaning there is no opening for them to perform *teshuvah*. Because, due to arrogance: (a) they justify their conduct, claiming it is actually good; (b) they find reasons and excuses for their conduct; (c) "love covers all transgressions." The common denominator is that *they do not* perform *teshuvah*.
*Matzah* signifies *bitul*, and as a result, *matzah* is spelled with the letter *hei*, which is open at the top. Because, due to *bitul*: (a) they do not justify their conduct; (b) they do not find reasons and excuses for their conduct; (c) when they see the not-good state in which they find themselves, they become *broken* by it.
When one is in a state of broken-heartedness, it is as the Rebbe Maharash said : "A Jewish sigh is *teshuvah ila'ah* (a higher level of repentance)." Consequently, in one "hour" (*sha'ah*), which also means "to turn" (*peneh*), in one "turn," they become a *tzaddik gamur*.

**H.** Based on this, we can explain what we find in the *Yerushalmi* : "The primary categories of labor are forty minus one... It is not written 'this thing' (*zeh hadavar*), but rather 'these are the things' (*eileh hadevarim*)... *Aleph* is one, *lamed* is thirty, *ches* is eight; the Sages do not refrain from interpreting [the letter *hei*] as a *ches*." Seemingly, this is difficult: it is true that letters from the same phonetic group are interchangeable, but how can one interpret the letter *hei* (of *eileh*) as the letter *ches* in the context of a *number*?
According to the above, we can explain this:
The thirty-nine labors of the *world* are, by their very *creation and formation*, a matter of *ches*, as mentioned above. However, through the service of "these are the things" (*eileh hadevarim*—which is the concept of the thirty-nine labors of the *Mishkan*)... "six days shall work be done" (automatically)—one transforms the thirty-nine labors of the world into the letter *hei*.
Therefore, in the verse that speaks of the service of "these are the things"—the thirty-nine labors as they were *in the Mishkan* (through which one transforms the *ches* into a *hei*)—the word *eileh* is written with the letter *hei*.

**I.** The Rebbe Rayatz related regarding the conduct of his father, the Rebbe (Rashab), whose *yahrzeit* (the 2nd of Nissan) falls in this week, concerning the recitation of the morning blessings.
The Rebbe said that he never heard how his father said "*Modeh Ani*," but he was accustomed to hearing how he said the morning blessings.
He related that while reciting the morning blessings: (a) the Rebbe Rashab would not wear a *gartel*; (b) he would look out the window.

**J.** The explanation of this in terms of a person's *avodah*:
The purpose of wearing a *gartel* is "Prepare to meet your G-d, O Israel", which is why "one must gird his loins when he prays", because before prayer ("to meet your G-d, O Israel"), there must be a *preparation* ("prepare") by the person.
But the recitation of the morning blessings, being immediately upon waking from sleep, before any *avodah*, in a state where one has only a "soul in his nostrils", can be done even without the preparation of wearing a *gartel*. Even in this state—without any preparation at all—it is within one's ability to recite the morning blessings!
This is similar to the content of reciting "*Modeh Ani*" *immediately* upon waking from sleep, without any prior preparations. Indeed, the recitation of "*Modeh Ani*" can be done even before washing hands, because the spirit of impurity does not have power over the "*Modeh Ani*" of a Jew. The recitation of the morning blessings, however, must be done specifically after washing hands. But after washing hands, one can recite them without additional preparations.
In this, the unique virtue of a Jew is expressed—that regardless of the fact that they have not yet prepared themselves at all for the service of Hashem, for they have "just now woken from sleep"... and have only a "soul in their nostrils" (as mentioned), nevertheless, after washing their hands and no longer having a connection to impurity, they have the power and ability to recite the morning blessings immediately!
There is another point here:
By wearing a *gartel*, one adds completeness to the matter of "separating between (his head and) his heart and his nakedness." As it applies to a person's *avodah*, this means that a separation and division is made between the mind and heart and physical, material matters (which are in the category of "nakedness"). As discussed above, a person's engagement in the affairs of this world must be only in the manner of "the labor of *your hands*", while their mind and heart must be immersed in lofty, spiritual matters.
Thus, even in a state where one lacks the *completeness* of the separation between their heart and "nakedness," and their mind and heart are immersed in physical and material matters—in the category of "the labor of *your head*" (and not "the labor of your hands" alone)—even then, one has the power to recite the morning blessings!

**K.** An even greater innovation is emphasized by the custom of the Rebbe Rashab *to look out the window* while reciting the morning blessings:
In addition to the fact that every Jew has the power to recite the morning blessings without any preparation on their part (as in section J), there is more: it is within their power and ability even to open a window and look out of it—as is explained in many places regarding the concept of looking, the aspect of "your eyes are like doves", etc..
Based on this, we can explain why the Rebbe Rashab transmitted these customs to his only son, the Rebbe Rayatz—because in these customs there is a lesson and a general instruction for *every* Jew, that in *any* state or situation they may be in, even when they have nothing but a "soul in their nostrils," and moreover, even when they are in the state of "the labor of your head," they have the power and ability to break through the wall, open a window, and connect with Hashem.

## B’ezras Hashem. Eve of Shabbos Kodesh, 2 Nissan, 5712.

[Following davening, the Rebbe gave the shaliach tzibbur, the esteemed Chassid Rabbi Sholom HaLevi Levitin, money for "mashke"—in accordance with his request, and while mentioning the adage that "everything is mashke"—as a contribution toward the Chassidic farbrengen in honor of the yom hilula. The Rebbe said:]

In connection with the yom hilula, a special kuntres has been published regarding the inner dimensions of Torah, which is the concept of the "mashke" (spirits) of Torah. As is known, the revealed aspect of Torah is compared to "water," while the inner dimension of Torah is compared to "wine" (and similarly, oil).

The main thing is that all of this—the learning, the farbrengen, and so on—should be in a manner that draws these matters down into actual practice.

May it be the will of Hashem—as is brought from the handwriting of the Tzemach Tzedek: "Our Rebbe of blessed memory asked (regarding bris milah, etc.) while drinking mashke"—that we merit to drink when it will be "all mashke."

This concept will be realized in the future Redemption, which is especially connected to the month of Nissan —the "month of *Geulah*".

[The Rebbe also mentioned the study of the chapters of Mishnayos corresponding to the letters of the name of the one whose yom hilula it is, that each person should study them himself, or that the chapters should be divided among the crowd.]

## With the help of Heaven. Shabbos Parshas Vayikra, 3 Nissan, 5712

(An unedited transcript)

"This people [am zu] I have created for Myself, they shall recount My praise". The Gemara states: "Let this [zeh] come and receive this [zot] from this [zeh] for this people [am zu], as it is said: 'This people [am zu] I have created...'" (this is according to the version in the *Ein Yaakov*). In the *Mechilta* on the verse "Until Your people [am zu] pass over, whom You have acquired," it is stated that the entire world is Yours, yet You have no people other than Yisrael, as it is said, "This people [am zu] I have created for Myself..." Yisrael are called a "possession" [kinyan], as it is said, "This people [am zu] You have acquired."

We must understand: what is the significance of this matter of "this" [zeh] and "this" [zu], regarding which they said, "Let this come and receive... for this people"? Before this, the Gemara there says, "Let the mighty [adir] come and receive... let the beloved [yadid] come... let the good [tov] come..." All these are titles of greatness (mighty, beloved, good). In contrast, regarding what is said, "Let this [zeh] come..." we must understand what the greatness is in the matter of "this."

Furthermore, we must understand that our Sages have said, "All the prophets prophesied with *koh* [thus], while Moshe prophesied with *zeh* [this]." This implies that only Moshe belongs to the level of *zeh*. If so, how can it be said that all of Yisrael are on the level of *zu*—*am zu*?

Additionally, we must understand why it says, "This people [am zu] I have created for Myself"—*zu* is a feminine form. It should have said *am zeh* (masculine), as it is written, "For this [zeh] nation [goy] is Your people." Why is it said, "This people [am zu] I have created," "This people [am zu] You have redeemed," "This people [am zu] You have acquired"—all in the feminine form?

2) To understand all this, we must first explain the aforementioned statement of our Sages that all the prophets prophesied with *koh*, while Moshe prophesied with *zeh*. The difference between the term *koh* and the term *zeh*, according to its simple meaning, is that the term *zeh* implies that "this is the thing as it is"—that one knows the thing precisely in all its details. The term *koh* implies that one does not know the thing in all its precise details, but only "thus" [koh]. This is the matter of all the prophets prophesying with *koh*: their prophecy was in the category of a "non-illuminating speculum", for they did not have the revelation below exactly as it is above. In contrast, Moshe prophesied with *zeh* because Moshe was below just as he was above in Atzilus, as is known. In Atzilus, "He and His life are one, and He and His essence are one". Thus it was with Moshe below as well: the Shechinah spoke from his throat. This is why his prophecy was with *zeh*.

The explanation of the matter is that the term *zeh* is appropriate only for matters of holiness. Regarding all other matters, it is impossible to point to them and say "this"—"this is the thing that is seen, and this is its entire essence"—since one does not see the essence of the thing, which is the purpose for which it was created. Rather, one sees only its externality, which is secondary to its essence and purpose. It is specifically in holiness that it is appropriate to use the term *zeh*, because in matters of holiness, the thing is not a medium for something else; rather, the purpose and the essence *is* the thing itself. In truth, the term *zeh* is applicable only to the *Atzmus* and essence of the Ein Sof, blessed be He, and everything connected to Him, for the *Atzmus* is "His existence is from His essence", and He is the "true existence". Therefore, it is appropriate to use the term *zeh* for Him, as He is the true existence. This is not the case for anything that is not the *Atzmus*; it is not appropriate to describe it with the term *zeh*, because it is not a true existence (since what one sees in it is not its essence, for its entire essence is from the side of the *Atzmus*).

3) However, by virtue of the *dveikus* (cleaving) that Yisrael and the Torah have with Elokus, the term *zeh* applies to them as well. This is because their *dveikus* is with the *Atzmus* of the Ein Sof, blessed be He, as it is written, "And you who cleave...", "and to cleave to Him", "and walk in His ways", and likewise through the Torah, for "Yisrael, the Torah, and the Holy One, blessed be He, are all one". Therefore, since the *Atzmus* is "His existence is from His essence," there exists in Yisrael as well a matter that is analogous to "His existence is from His essence", and thus the term *zeh* is applicable to them.

However, the Holy One, blessed be He, does not want the matters of Yisrael to come about without their own *avodah*, in the manner of "bread of shame". Rather, He wants all matters to be through the *avodah* of man and specifically through free choice. Therefore, "G-d made this [zeh] corresponding to this [zeh]"[ftnref_526_23, 527_24]. He created this physical world, where it appears that its existence is from its own essence, to the point that it is full of *kelipos* and *sitra achra* that say, "My river is my own, and I have made it for myself", "I, and there is none else besides me". Through the *avodah* of Yisrael in this world to reveal the *Atzmus*, they then possess the term *zeh* through their own *avodah*, and not in the manner of "bread of shame."

Therefore (since the creation of the *sitra achra* is only for the sake of the *avodah* of Yisrael), even the *yetzer hara* itself knows that "there is none else besides Him". It is only given permission to confuse man and entice him, so that "whoever wishes to err may err" and imagine that his existence is from his own essence. The intent of this is so that by standing in the test, the reward and the revelation will be even more sublime. This is like the parable of the harlot in the *Zohar*, Parshas Terumah: when the harlot entices the king's son and the king's son stands in the test, through this he increases his reward. The meaning of "increasing the reward" is not that it is an increase in quantity—that it is larger, but still not the true reward—but rather that through this, a reward is created so great that there is none greater: the connection to the *Atzmus* that is above all lights and revelations. Regarding this it is said, "Great is Hashem and highly praised in the city of our G-d"—the greatness of the *Atzmus*. By man standing in the test and not being affected by the claim of the *sitra achra* that his existence is from his own essence—the category of *zeh*—specifically through this he reaches the revelation of the matter of the *Atzmus* that is within Yisrael, the connection with the *Atzmus*, until they are called by the name *am zu*.

4) The connection with the *Atzmus* is specifically through Moshe Rabbeinu. As it is stated in the Gemara there: "'Let *zeh* come'—*zeh* is Moshe, as it is written regarding him, 'For this [zeh] man, Moshe...'" This means that Moshe is the one who connects Yisrael and draws into them the category of *zeh*, because Moshe is the one who connects worlds and souls with Elokus. And the "extension of Moshe in every generation"—these are the "heads of the thousands of Yisrael" in every generation, who are the ones that connect Yisrael with the *Atzmus* and draw into them the category of *zeh*. As explained at length in *Tanya*, even the unlearned and even the sinners who rebel against the Torah scholars—their connection with Elokus is through the "heads of the thousands of Yisrael," just as a child connects to the father through the father's mind. And this is: "Let *zeh* come"—*zeh* is Moshe—"and receive *zot*"—*zot* is the Torah—"from *zeh*"—*zeh* is "This [zeh] is my G-d and I will glorify Him"—"for this people [am zu]." Moshe is the one who draws into Yisrael, through the Torah, the category of *zeh*, and through the fulfillment of *mitzvos*, they are in the category of *zeh*.

5) However, the category of *zeh* as it is in Yisrael is stated in the feminine form, *am zu*. The explanation of this is that the connection to the *Atzmus* of the Blessed One through the study of Torah and the fulfillment of *mitzvos* is specifically when a person has no personal feeling in it. When there is understanding in *taam v'daas* (reason and knowledge) or feeling—even a feeling of holiness—then his fulfillment of *mitzvos* is not in a manner where he is connected with the *Atzmus*, for there is some "thing" here outside of the *Atzmus*. It is similar to what is explained in *Tanya* that even one who is a *tzaddik* and serves Hashem with love and fear and with "great love in delights," he is still in the category of "there is one who loves." Since there is an existence here that is outside of the *Atzmus*, he cannot be connected with the *Atzmus*.

The connection to the *Atzmus* through the study of Torah and the fulfillment of *mitzvos* is specifically when the study and fulfillment are without any ulterior motives, without *taam v'daas*, without feeling, and without motives of holiness. Through this, he is brought out of his bad state, but only through *kabbalas ol* (acceptance of the yoke). This means that nothing "touches" him; he only wants to fulfill the Higher Will. His fulfillment of *mitzvos* is in such a way that the easiest of the easy *mitzvos* and the most severe of the severe *mitzvos* are equal to him. Even though there are *mitzvos* that are "easiest of the easy" to him—whose fulfillment does not affect him as much—and *mitzvos* that are "most severe of the severe" to him—which lift him from the lowest depths—nevertheless, to him, the easiest of the easy and the most severe of the severe are equal. Since his fulfillment of *mitzvos* is not from *taam v'daas* but from *kabbalas ol*—that he wants to fulfill the Higher Will—then everything is equal, because from the perspective of the Higher Will, there is no difference between the easiest of the easy and the most severe of the severe.

It is known that the Will is distinct from the internal powers of the soul. In all internal powers—and even in the power of the intellect, which is the most superior of the internal powers—there is division. For example, regarding the intellect, it is possible to perform only one detail, and thereby fulfill what the intellect necessitated in its part. This is not the case with the Will; there is no division in it. If one does not fulfill even one detail of the Will, the entire matter of the Will is lacking. The fulfillment of the Will is in one of two ways: either one fulfills all the details of the Will, in which case he completes the Will, or he lacks even one detail—even a minor one—in the fulfillment of the Will, in which case the entire Will is lacking. The reason for this is that the Will is a category of *makif* (encompassing), which is a highly superior encompassing power and is not connected with internal matters. From this, it is understood for our context as well: from the perspective of the Higher Will, there is no difference between the easiest of the easy and the most severe of the severe. If he wants to fulfill the Higher Will, it does not matter to him if this *mitzvah* is one of the easiest of the easy or one of the most severe of the severe.

Within this itself, there are two levels. It is possible for the fulfillment of *mitzvos* to be by way of *kabbalas ol* like a simple servant. Higher than that (as explained above) is the category of a "son who is a servant." This has an advantage over a mere servant, because the servant only fulfills the master's will but does not feel the will and does not know its reason, and therefore it is no great feat that he does everything with *kabbalas ol*. In contrast, the category of "son" is that he feels the will and knows the reason for the will, yet his fulfillment of the will is not because of the reason, but only to fulfill the master's will. Specifically through the category of *kabbalas ol* of a servant, one connects with the *Atzmus*. Therefore, it is said *am zu* in the feminine form, because his entire matter is that he is in the category of a "receiver"—a female—who is only a vessel for reception and performs his *avodah* with *kabbalas ol*, and through this, he connects with the *Atzmus*.

6) This is what the verse continues: "This people [am zu] I have created for Myself, they shall recount My praise." Specifically when the *avodah* is in the category of *am zu*—the category of *kabbalas ol*—then specifically "they shall recount My praise," meaning that he recounts the praise of the Holy One, blessed be He, according to the intent. When the *avodah* is according to *taam v'daas*, there is some existence of the person performing the *avodah*. Even if he is a perfect *tzaddik* who loves Hashem with "great love in delights," he is still "one who loves," and he must contemplate that he is standing before the King. Since he is an "existence," this is not according to the intent; on the contrary, "one who points out to me, pointing before the King...". This is not the case when his *avodah* is in the category of *kabbalas ol* completely; then "they shall recount My praise" according to the intent, because he is then connected with the *Atzmus*.

With this, we can also understand why the verse here uses the term *am* (people), *am zu*, even though the term *am* indicates a very low level, as is known. For this is the innovation of *kabbalas ol*: that even one who is at the lowest level, nevertheless, specifically through *kabbalas ol*, he connects with the category of *zeh* and becomes in the category of *am zu*. And even though "all the prophets prophesied with *koh*," this is when we are speaking of the definition of a level. From the perspective of the level of the prophets—even though it is a highly superior level, for "the spirit of Hashem spoke in them and His word was on their tongue"—nevertheless, because they are at a level of revelation, they only reach the category of *koh*, and therefore they prophesied with *koh*. But through *kabbalas ol*, which is an *avodah* that is not within the definition of a level—for from the perspective of the matter of *kabbalas ol*, the greatest of the great and the simplest of the simple are equal, and therefore also in the manner of *avodah*, the easiest of the easy and the most severe of the severe are equal—through this they draw from the category of *zeh*, the *Atzmus* of the Blessed One.

7) There are several modes in the revelation of the category of *zeh*. The revelation of the category of *zeh* as it is in this time is not yet in absolute perfection. Even at the splitting of the Red Sea, when "a maidservant saw at the sea what Yechezkel [the greatest of the prophets] did not see"[ftnref_548_45, 549_46], nevertheless, the term *zeh* was said only once: "This [zeh] is my G-d and I will glorify Him." However, in the time to come, in the days of Moshiach and the resurrection of the dead, they will say *zeh* twice, as it is written, "And it shall be said in that day, 'Behold, this [zeh] is our G-d... this [zeh] is Hashem, we have hoped for Him.'" As it is stated in the *Midrash*: The Holy One, blessed be He, said, "In this world, you said before Me once, 'This [zeh] is my G-d,' but in the time to come, you will say the same thing twice, as it is said, 'And it shall be said in that day, Behold, this [zeh] is our G-d... this [zeh] is Hashem...'"

It is explained in the *maamar* of the *Baal HaHillula* (the Rebbe Rashab)[ftnref_552_49, 553_50] that "Behold, this [zeh] is our G-d" is *Ze'er Anpin*, and "this [zeh] is Hashem, we have hoped for Him" is *Atik Kaddisha*. The explanation for this is that *Ze'er Anpin* is a matter of smallness and *tzimtzum*. Now, in this world, the revelation of the category of *zeh* is only once—that is, in *tzimtzum*—meaning the revelation is only in comprehension and not in the category of tangible sight. Therefore, even though at the splitting of the Red Sea it is written, "This [zeh] is my G-d..." and our Sages said, "a maidservant saw...", nevertheless, the sea was split into twelve sections corresponding to the twelve tribes, meaning that each tribe had a path of its own. And even though it is written, "Then Moshe and the children of Yisrael sang" (with the letter *vav* connecting them), nevertheless, there were differences between them. This is because now the revelation is only in comprehension and not in the tangible, for the revelation does not pertain to the physical senses—sight of the eye, hearing of the ear, smell of the nose, and speech of the mouth.

In contrast, in the time to come, there will also be the matter of "this [zeh] is Hashem"—the revelation of *Atik Kaddisha*—meaning the revelation of the category of Hashem that is above all definitions, the category of "He was, He is, and He will be as one", and above that, the category of Hashem that has no "points," meaning He is above "points". This revelation will be tangible, such that everyone will see with their physical finger and say, "This [zeh] is Hashem"—this is *Arich* and this is *Atik*. All this will be in the time to come, speedily in our days, when the redemption from Egypt will be in the physical, and consequently, there will be the redemption from Egypt in the spiritual—for the descent of the soul below is the matter of the exile of Egypt in the spiritual, as is known—speedily in our days, through Moshiach Tzidkeinu, "And all flesh shall see together that the mouth of Hashem has spoken".

## B’s”d. Sunday, 11 Nissan 5712.

**Unedited**

1. [Following the instruction of the Rebbe, members of the "Committee for Organizing the Review of Chassidus" entered his holy room, and he said to them:]

Convey to all the students—convey it orally to those present here, and in writing to those who have traveled—that they should utilize the days of the Pesach holiday, the days before Pesach, and the days after Pesach, to review Chassidus in the shuls regarding the themes of the day: Yetzias Mitzrayim, Krias Yam Suf, and the like—each shul according to its nature. This includes words of hisorerus regarding Yiddishkeit and regarding Moshiach—each person according to the matters they are capable of—and the main thing is that they should be occupied with this ("m'zal tan").

They should not think that by traveling from here they are exempt from this avodah; on the contrary, the avodah that was done here, each one must utilize and bring to their own location.

I wish to receive a detailed report of each person's activity—when and where they spoke on the first day of Pesach, the second day of Pesach, Shabbos Chol HaMoed Pesach, Shevi'i shel Pesach, and Acharon shel Pesach, as well as the days before and after Pesach.

It is worthwhile that the students first announce their plan, and afterwards report what they actually did, and you shall report it to me.

You may convey these words in my name, so that it should be done with extra alacrity.

May Hashem grant that it should be with success.

*

2. [Following the instruction of the Rebbe, two minyanim of the elders of Anash and the Temimim, who were listed by name, entered his room, and he recited before them the maamar beginning with the words "Adonai Sifasai Tiftach" (Hashem, open my lips...).]

*

3. [In response to a group of Anash who presented their proposal and request that the Rebbe recite a maamar every Shabbos, and that he would kindly provide the maamarim in writing in order to print them, the Rebbe said:]

I do indeed wish to print the maamarim, but there is no free time for this, especially since there are many who are waiting for a response to their letters (as the activists among the elders of Anash also claim)...

(And afterwards he said:) In general, why do you demand from me? Demand from yourselves the matters that you need to do ("What are you demanding from me? Demand from yourselves the matters that you need to do")!

------- * -------

In the chapter of Tehillim for this year (upon which the maamar was recited)—"Make me hear joy and gladness," and the Rebbe responded and added (with a smile): "The bones which You have crushed shall rejoice."

## B’ezras Hashem. 11 Nissan, 5712

(Unedited transcript)

"Hashem, open my lips, and my mouth shall declare Your praise." This verse is the request of King David, the request of the Congregation of Israel, and the request of every individual Jew. It was established to be recited before prayer, as the Gemara states: Rabbi Yochanan says, "Initially, one says, 'Hashem, open my lips.'" We must understand why it was ordained to be said specifically before the Amidah prayer, and not before the entire prayer service (i.e., before Krias Shema and its blessings, and before Pesukei D’zimra). Even more difficult to understand is the fact that the Gemara there relates that Rav Bruna joined the redemption (Geulah) to the prayer (Tefillah) and did not cease laughing [with joy] all day. The Gemara challenges this: How could he join them, given that Rabbi Yochanan says, "Initially, one says, 'Hashem, open my lips'"? The Gemara answers that since the Sages established it [as part of the prayer], it is considered a "long prayer" (Tefillah Arichta). Seemingly, since joining Geulah to Tefillah is such a lofty matter, why did they establish the recitation of this verse before the Shemoneh Esrei—which then necessitates the excuse that it is considered a "long prayer"—rather than establishing it before the entire prayer service? If they had done so, the joining of Geulah to Tefillah would be simple and straightforward.

It is known that joining Geulah to Tefillah is the concept of Yehuda approaching Yosef. Yehuda represents Tefillah, as it is written, "This time I will thank (odeh) Hashem," and in the Sefiros, this refers to the Sefirah of Malchus, which descends into the worlds of Beriah, Yetzirah, and Asiyah to perform *birurim* (refinements), as it is written, "And she gives food to her household." Yosef represents Geulah and salvation—the level of Yesod, which bestows influence upon the Sefirah of Malchus, as it is written, "And Yosef... was the provider for all the people of the land." This influence continues even when the Sefirah of Malchus is in a state of descent into the lower worlds, just as it was with Yosef, who, during the seven years of famine, prepared grain even for the years of hunger—meaning his influence persisted even during the years of famine. The concept of joining Geulah to Tefillah is the connection between Yesod (of Ze’er Anpin) and Malchus. With this, we can understand why joining Geulah to Tefillah is specifically before the Shemoneh Esrei: the innovation of the Shemoneh Esrei, compared to the parts of the prayer that precede it, is that the Shemoneh Esrei is the *yichud* (unification) of *Zon* (Ze’er Anpin and Nukvah). As explained in many places, Krias Shema is the *yichud* of *Abba* and *Imma*, while Shemoneh Esrei is the *yichud* of *Zon*. This is also the reason why the Shemoneh Esrei is recited while standing—in the "Atzilus of the world of standing"—whereas Krias Shema is recited while sitting, in the level of "your dwellings" (the worlds of Beriah, Yetzirah, and Asiyah). Although, on the face of it, Krias Shema is higher than Shemoneh Esrei (since Krias Shema is the *yichud* of *Abba* and *Imma*), the fact is that precisely in the lower worlds—the level of "your dwellings," where the revelation is in a state of descent and humility (rather than in its ultimate strength)—one can achieve the *yichud* of *Abba* and *Imma* without the nullification of existence, which is not the case in Shemoneh Esrei. Therefore, we join Geulah to Tefillah, so that from the *yichud* of *Abba* and *Imma* in the blessings of Krias Shema (Geulah), influence is drawn into the *yichud* of *Zon* in the Shemoneh Esrei, so that the ascent in the Shemoneh Esrei may be as it should be. According to this, it is even more puzzling why they established the recitation of the verse "Hashem, open my lips" between Geulah and Tefillah, which forces the excuse that it is considered a "long prayer," rather than establishing it at the very beginning of the prayer (before Hodu).

**2.** All of this will be understood by first explaining the concept of prayer. The general difference between Torah and prayer is that the concept of Torah is "from above to below"—meaning it does not involve the service of the lower beings, and requires only the preparation of the vessel so that the vessel does not obstruct the flow of light. Prayer, however, is "from below to above," where the influence is commensurate with the level of one's service. The influence from above is through the Sefiros (the *Midos*), as it is written, "Six days Hashem made" (it does not say "in six days"), referring to the six supernal *Midos* from which the world was built. This influence is achieved through man's service in refining his *Midos* through his work in thought, speech, and action, which effects a refinement of his own *Midos*. It is similar on a higher level: through the prayer "May it be Your will," one effects the drawing down of a new will from above. From the general concept, it is understood that the nature of prayer is specifically a process of ascent from below to above. However, in prayer, there is a higher level that is like Torah—the level of *Tefillah Arichta* (a long prayer)—where the influence drawn down by this prayer is "from above to below." Just as in Torah, although the general concept of Torah is "from above to below," there are nevertheless two levels within Torah itself: the level of the "dew of Torah," which is "from above to below" (as the saying goes, "Dew is not restrained," for dew does not depend on the service of the lower beings), and the level of the "rain of Torah," which is "from below to above" (for the concept of rain is drawn down through the ascent from below, as it is written, "And a mist would rise from the earth and water..."). So too in prayer, there are two levels: although the general nature of prayer is "from below to above," it also contains a level of influence "from above to below."

**3.** The explanation of this is based on the Sages' teaching that "Whoever reads and reviews, the Holy One, Blessed be He, reads and reviews opposite him." In another place, it is stated that during the first three hours of the night, the Holy One, Blessed be He, sits and engages in Torah. It is explained elsewhere that the concept of "whoever reads and reviews..." refers to the level of Torah that is "from below to above," where the influence of Torah is through the person's study below, which depends on man's service. However, the concept of the first three hours is the influence "from above to below" through an *is'arusa d'leila* (arousal from above), which does not depend on the actions of the lower beings. Just as it is in the Torah above, so it is in the Torah below: there are two modes of Torah study—studying Torah "not for its own sake" and studying Torah "for its own sake." For even regarding study "not for its own sake," the Sages said, "A person should always study Torah, even if not for its own sake, for from [studying] not for its own sake, one comes to [studying] for its own sake." The concept of studying "not for its own sake" at a higher level is that even though one studies Torah properly, innovates in Torah, and aims for the truth, the study is still "not for its own sake" because he feels that *he* is the one studying and *he* is the one innovating. However, the concept of studying "for its own sake" means that he does not feel himself to be an existence at all; rather, his study is like one who repeats after the reader—he is saying the word of Hashem (he says what is being said above). Similarly, this can be understood regarding prayer: although its general nature is through the actions of the lower beings, there is a level in prayer that does not depend on the actions of the lower beings, but rather the person is in a state of total *bitul* (nullification), and his prayer is merely like one who repeats after the reader.

**4.** This is the essence of the request, "Hashem, open my lips," which is the concept of *Tefillah Arichta*. As the simple meaning suggests, he asks the Almighty to open his lips, "and my mouth shall declare Your praise"—"declare" (*yagid*) comes from the root of *haggadah* (telling) and *hemshech* (drawing down). Because through his *bitul*, he reaches such a level that this is not his (man's) prayer, but rather "my mouth shall declare *Your* praise"—Your praise. Through prayer in this manner, the influence from above is also such that the flow cannot be delayed; rather, it is drawn down below just as it is above. In the first level of prayer, which is "from below to above," it is possible for there to be a delay in the influence drawn down by the prayer. This is not the case through prayer at this level, *Tefillah Arichta*, where the influence is drawn down and arrives below at that very time and in that very place, immediately.

**5.** With this, we can understand why we recite the verse "Hashem, open my lips" specifically before the Shemoneh Esrei. The twelve middle blessings of the Shemoneh Esrei are prayers for material sustenance—that He should bless the years and heal the sick. In order for influence to be drawn down into the physical as it is [above], there must be the level of *Tefillah Arichta* as mentioned above. According to Kabbalah, at the beginning of the Shemoneh Esrei we say, "The Almighty (Kel Elyon)... and brings a redeemer to their children's children." It is explained elsewhere that "brings a redeemer" refers to the drawing down of new *Netzach, Hod, and Yesod* (Nehi), which is achieved through *Kel Elyon*—the influence from the level of Keter. It is also stated in the writings of the Arizal regarding the interpretation of the verse "Hashem, open my lips" that "Hashem" (Ad-noy) is the level of Malchus, "my lips" are the level of Netzach and Hod, "open" is the level of Tiferes, and "my mouth" are the new Nehi. This concept comes from the level of Keter, which is the *Aleph* in *Ad-noy*, as explained in the Siddur—that the primary focus is on the *Aleph* in *Ad-noy*, for *Aleph* is *pela* (wonder), the level of the supernal wonder, which is the level of Keter that is above the level of Chochma and Binah, and its influence is in Binah, as the saying goes, "The revelation of Atik in Binah." This is the concept of "a fifty-year-old to counsel," for "counsel" refers to the "counseling kidneys," which are the level of Netzach and Hod—meaning new Nehi—which are drawn down specifically through the level of the fifty gates of Binah, the fiftieth gate that is above the *Seder Hishtalshelus* (order of evolution). As it is said regarding this, "To the upper ones it seems to be below, and to the lower ones it seems to be above"—the meaning of which is that it is above the definition of "above" and "below," and from this is drawn the level of new Nehi. This is also the case according to Chassidus: through the recitation of "Hashem, open my lips," which is the *bitul*, the level of *Tefillah Arichta*, one draws down the material influences in the twelve middle blessings of the Shemoneh Esrei, which is the primary intent of prayer.

**6.** This is "Hashem, open my lips, and my mouth shall declare Your praise"—this is the concept of *bitul*, where he asks that the Almighty open his lips, and "my mouth shall declare *Your* praise," meaning that he is repeating after the Reader, only drawing down the praise from above. When he is in a state of total *bitul*, he prays with a bitter soul, as the psalm continues: "Create for me a clean heart, O G-d, and renew a steadfast spirit within me. Do not cast me away from Your presence, and do not take Your holy spirit from me. Restore to me the joy of Your salvation, and let a generous spirit uphold me. Then I will teach transgressors Your ways, and sinners will return to You." Through this, one draws down from the level of the supernal wonder, and the influence from there does not come through constraints and boundaries, but is drawn down below immediately. Therefore, we precede the recitation of the verse "Hashem, open my lips" to the Shemoneh Esrei, because through this, the prayer is in the state of *Tefillah Arichta*, and through this, we draw down the influences in the physical, etc.

## B's"d. 13 Nissan 5712.

### B's"d. 13 Nissan 5712.

*Unedited*

1. After Maariv, the Rebbe said that a farbrengen should certainly be held in connection with the hilula of the Tzemach Tzedek (and asked how the arrangements were made last year). He gave a ten-dollar bill as a contribution toward the farbrengen, and said that they should come to him so he could exchange this bill for ten individual ones, adding with a smile that in this way it would be more worthwhile ("azoy vet zich mer loynen").

*

2. On the day of 13 Nissan, the Rebbe inquired about the details regarding the baking of matzos on Erev Pesach, as well as the appointment of the bochurim who would supervise the baking process. When the mashpia remarked during the discussion that the task of supervision could be performed by anyone, the Rebbe responded with astonishment, saying: "Is that so?! You have innovated a new concept in avodah — that anyone can supervise!..."

## B's"d. Wednesday, 14 Nissan, Erev Pesach, 5712.

**Unedited**

Following Mincha, before beginning the distribution of matzos, the Rebbe instructed that the nigun "A-li Atah" be sung (and he repeated—evidently because they did not begin singing immediately—that if they did not sing, he would not distribute the matzos).

During the distribution of the matzos, the Rebbe blessed each person with a kosher and joyous Pesach ("A kosher'n un a freilichen Pesach").

To one individual, he gave two pieces of matzah, saying: This is for "michla d'meheimanusa" (food of faith), and this is for "michla d'asvasa" (food of healing). And the two are interdependent: when there is "michla d'meheimanusa," there will also be "michla d'asvasa," and consequently, there will be complete health.

[To a doctor who asked what the concept behind this was, the Rebbe said:] When a doctor gives medicine to a patient, the patient does not ask questions; rather, he takes the medicine, even when he does not understand how it works...

To one person he said: Matzah represents Elokus, and the eating of matzah means "that one eats Elokus", and this concept applies not only on the night of the Seder, but throughout all the days of Pesach.

He asked one person if he had been present during the baking of the matzos (the Rebbe’s own matzos, from which he was distributing), and when the person replied that he had been present but (for some reason) had not assisted in the work of the baking, the Rebbe gave him an additional piece, saying: This is for the presence at the baking ("far dem zein") in and of itself.

## Sicha of the First Night of Pesach, 5712

**A. "In all other nights we eat either sitting or reclining; tonight we are all reclining":**

The Rebbe Rayatz explains in a Sicha that "reclining" (*mesubin*) refers to the world of Atzilus. The meaning of "tonight we are all reclining" is that on the Seder night (the first night in Eretz Yisrael, and the first and second nights in the Diaspora), all of Bnei Yisrael ("all of us") are at the level of the world of Atzilus ("reclining").

We must explain this concept in terms of *avodas ha'adam*—as with all matters of Torah, and Chassidus in particular, which must be translated into actual *avodah* in thought, speech, and action.

In his explanation in Iggeres HaKodesh regarding the superiority of the world of Atzilus over the worlds of Biyah, the Alter Rebbe notes that in Atzilus, "He and His life are one, and He and His attributes are one," whereas this is not the case in the worlds of Biyah. He further explains regarding *neshamos*: "Similarly, the soul of man, which emerged from the union of the *Zon* of Atzilus before descending into Biyah, is not considered a separate entity in its own right, but is rather akin to a manifestation of Elokus... similar to the *keilim* of the *Sefiros* of Atzilus." He adds that "even after the *nefesh, ruach, neshamah* of Atzilus descended into this world, for the early *tzaddikim*, it is possible that their essence did not change into something separate from Elokus; therefore, they would depart [from this world] when they wished, before they could sin." From this, we understand that the level of the world of Atzilus in *avodas ha'adam* is the **negation of any connection to sin.**

This is the meaning of "In all other nights... whether sitting (Biyah) or reclining; tonight we are all reclining (Atzilus)." Throughout the entire year (in all other nights), there are differences in level among Bnei Yisrael regarding their connection to sin ("whether sitting or reclining") according to their standing and situation in the "choice of life" (as it is written, "See, I have set before you today life and good, and death and evil... therefore choose life"), as every person knows their own standing and situation. However, on the night of Pesach ("tonight"), all Bnei Yisrael ("all of us") are in a lofty level where they have **no connection to sin at all**, which is the level of the world of Atzilus ("reclining").

**B. The reason for this is the magnitude of the elevation of "tonight":**

It is written, "And I will pass through the land of Egypt this night... I am Hashem." "I, and not an angel... I, and not a seraph... I, and not a messenger... I, and no other." It is explained in the discourses of Chassidus that these four levels—angel, seraph, messenger, and "other"—correspond to the four worlds of Abiya (from bottom to top, from Asiyah to Atzilus). This means that "I, and no other" excludes even the level of the world of Atzilus. For the revelation that occurs "this night" is from the **Essence and Being of the Ein Sof, Baruch Hu**, which is above even the world of Atzilus.

The reason it was necessary to have a revelation of His Essence and Being specifically is that in Egypt, Bnei Yisrael were sunken in the forty-nine gates of impurity,. This is the deepest descent possible for Bnei Yisrael (as the descent to the fiftieth gate of impurity does not apply to Bnei Yisrael). To extract them from there, the revelation of the four worlds of Abiya—angel, seraph, messenger, and "other"—was insufficient. Furthermore, on the contrary: had an angel or the like descended, it would, G-d forbid, have been swallowed up within the *klipah* ("it would have become a ruin itself"). Therefore, it was necessary for there to be a revelation of His Essence and Being ("I, and no other"), which is above even the world of Atzilus, through which alone they could be extracted and elevated from the deepest descent of the impurity of Egypt.

By this power, the standing and situation of "tonight we are all reclining" is recreated every single year. Through the revelation of the Essence on this night, all Bnei Yisrael are elevated to the level of the world of Atzilus, in which there is no connection to sin (regardless of their standing and situation "in all other nights," *kal v'chomer* from the standing and situation of Bnei Yisrael in the depths of the descent in the land of Egypt).

**C. It should be added that the elevation of "tonight we are all reclining" comes after the preamble of "This is the bread of affliction that our fathers ate in the land of Egypt":**

"Bread of affliction" (*lachma anya*) points to the *avodah* of *kabbalas ol* (acceptance of the yoke), which lacks the taste and *taanug* of intellectual understanding and comprehension. This is the meaning of "This is the bread of affliction that our fathers ate in the land of Egypt"—the *avodah* of Bnei Yisrael while they were in Egypt (when they were not capable of understanding and comprehension) was in the manner of *kabbalas ol*. Precisely through this, they merited the most lofty revelation "this night," for the drawing down and revelation of the Essence is achieved specifically through *kabbalas ol*.

Following this comes the conclusion of the paragraph, "Next year, may we be free men," which alludes to the revelation of the Essence (which is achieved through the *avodah* of *kabbalas ol*) in the time to come.

This is the content of the preamble "Ha Lachma Anya" to "Tonight we are all reclining"—that the standing and situation of "all of us are reclining" due to the revelation of the Essence "this night" is achieved through the *avodah* of *kabbalas ol*, and through this, we also merit the revelation of the Essence in the time to come.

*

**D. "Initially, our fathers were idol worshipers, and now the Omnipresent has brought us close to His service":**

One must understand the reason for recounting the disgrace ("one begins with the disgrace," "initially our fathers were idol worshipers")—is this not the opposite of the rule that the Torah does not speak in derogatory terms even regarding an impure animal?

It can be said that the story of the disgrace ("initially they were idol worshipers...") is relevant to the *avodas ha'adam* "this night":

The Alter Rebbe explains in Tanya that "a person who transgresses the will of the Almighty," even with a "minor transgression," is "far worse and more inferior than the *Sitra Achra* and *klipah* called 'idolatry' and 'other gods,' and he is in a state of total separation from the unity and oneness of Hashem, more so than it, and it is as if he denies His oneness more than it, G-d forbid."

Since a person knows their own standing and situation—that not only do they fail in matters of indulgence, but sometimes they can even fail in forbidden matters (inadvertently or even intentionally), which is a matter of idolatry, as mentioned above—they are liable to fall into despair.

To this, we say: "Initially, our fathers were idol worshipers... and now the Omnipresent has brought us close to His service." Even if in the moment before ("initially") one was in a standing and situation of being an "idol worshiper" in a subtle sense, in one hour and one moment, one can be transformed from one extreme to the other (from a completely wicked person to a completely righteous person), "now (in this moment) the Omnipresent has brought us close to His service."

This is emphasized most strongly "this night":

The ultimate descent of Bnei Yisrael while they were in the land of Egypt was to the standing and situation of "idol worshipers," as our Sages say, "These were idol worshipers and those were idol worshipers." And yet, "this night" there was a revelation of the Essence for them (as mentioned in sections B-C), the ultimate perfection of "the Omnipresent has brought us close..."

It is similar every single year—even if before this ("initially") one was in a standing and situation of idolatry ("initially they were idol worshipers") in a subtle sense at least, when "this night" arrives, one is **guaranteed** [a certainty that is more than hope, as the Rebbe Rayatz explains in the Sicha regarding the superiority of *bitachon* over hope—that one does not merely hope it will be so, but is *certain* that it will surely be so] that "now the Omnipresent has brought us close to His service," for through the revelation of the Essence "this night," one can change in one hour ("in one turn") from one essence to another.

*

**E. "If He had only brought us out of Egypt... it would have been enough for us":**

The Rebbe Rayatz said that one should not pause while reciting the fourteen (or fifteen) "Dayenu"s. From this, it is understood that all the details mentioned in "Dayenu" are connected to one another, to the point that they are a single matter.

The explanation for this in *avodas ha'adam* is as follows:

As a preamble—all the details in "Dayenu" exist also in the personal *avodah* of every single Jew (in addition to the simple meaning regarding the Jewish people as a whole)—from the first matter, "He brought us out of Egypt," to the last matter, "He built for us the Chosen House," and the other matters in between.

"If He had only brought us out of Egypt" refers to the lowest level in *avodas ha'adam*—being in "Egypt," which includes (in addition to the Egypt of *kedushah*) also the Egypt of the *Sitra Achra*, and one must be brought out of there.

"He built... the Chosen House" refers to the highest level in *avodas ha'adam*—the building of the private Beis HaMikdash within oneself, as it is written, "And I will dwell within them"—"within him" is not said, but "within them," within each and every one,—up to the revelation of the "essence of the Shechinah" that is in the Sanctuary (as explained in the discourse *Basi LeGani*), which refers to the Essence and Being of the Ein Sof, Baruch Hu.

Between them exists a "Seder Hishtalshelus" (order of progression) of many levels—which are all the details of "Dayenu" between "He brought us out of Egypt" and "He built... the Chosen House."

Despite the vast difference in value and the gap of many levels from the exodus from Egypt to the building of the Chosen House, they are connected to one another, to the point that they are a single matter, which is why one does not pause between them—for at the exodus from Egypt, one is already connected to the building of the Chosen House.

**F. The explanation for this:**

Even when a Jew is sunken in the forty-nine gates of impurity, G-d forbid, this is only **in the revealed and external sense**—in actual practice, in action, speech, thought, emotions, and intellect, and even in the *external* aspect of the will. But the **inner** aspect of their will, a desire that is above will (as is known that *ratzon* is the external aspect of the will, and *chefetz* is the inner aspect of the will), is in *kedushah*.

As we find in the revealed Torah—"We coerce him until he says 'I want to'". The Rambam explains the reason: "He wants to be a Jew and wants to perform all the mitzvos and distance himself from transgressions, and it is his evil inclination that overpowers him. Once he is beaten until his inclination is weakened and he says 'I want to,' it is 'of his own free will'"—meaning that through the coercion, his true will (*chefetz*) is revealed.

From this, it is understood that even when a Jew is in Egypt, by virtue of the **inner aspect of their will** (*chefetz*), they are connected to the most lofty level of the building of the Chosen House, and the lack is only in the revelation of this *chefetz* in the external and practical sense as well.

Therefore, when the *revelation* of the inner will begins—that one *wants* to leave Egypt (by saying "If He had *only* brought us out of Egypt... it would have been enough for us"), it also reveals their connection (which is by virtue of their inner will) to the building of the Chosen House (even though one must pass through many levels until the building of the Chosen House in actual *avodah*).

*

**G. "Next year in Jerusalem":**

As a preamble—our Rebbes, the Nesi'im, would explain (primarily) only the first part of the Haggadah (until *Birchas HaMazon*), and not (as much) the second part of the Haggadah.

The reason for this is known—that the first part of the Haggadah relates to the redemption from Egypt, and the second part of the Haggadah relates (primarily) to the future redemption. Therefore, they explained the first part regarding the matters of the redemption from Egypt that had already occurred in actuality, whereas the matters of the future redemption (in the second part), which had not yet come in revelation, they did not explain.

However, based on what is known—that the revelations of the time to come depend on our actions and *avodah* throughout the duration of the exile—it is understood that the matters of the future redemption *that are relevant to our avodah now* must be explained and clarified.

Similarly, regarding the paragraph "Next year in Jerusalem" at the conclusion and seal of the Seder: in addition to the simple meaning regarding the future redemption, there is also a matter here that relates to our *avodah* now, which brings about and effects the redemption, as follows.

**H. The meaning of "Next year in Jerusalem"—simply—is that we pray and bless that by next year the future redemption will have already occurred, and consequently, we will already be in Jerusalem.**

But according to this, the precision of "(Next year) **in Jerusalem**" specifically is not understood, rather than "in Eretz Yisrael" (for when Mashiach Tzidkeinu comes, not all Bnei Yisrael will be in Jerusalem specifically, but also in all the cities of Eretz Yisrael), as we say at the beginning of the Haggadah, "This year we are here; next year in the **land of Israel**" (and not "in Jerusalem")?

Simply put, one could say that the precision of "in Jerusalem" is because on Pesach, all Bnei Yisrael would be in Jerusalem due to the obligation of the pilgrimage (*aliyah l'regel*). However, this answer is insufficient, because (in addition to the fact that this raises the question regarding the paragraph at the beginning of the Haggadah, "next year in the land of Israel," and not in Jerusalem), also at the conclusion and seal of Yom Kippur we say "Next year in Jerusalem," even though Yom Kippur is not one of the three festivals where there is the obligation of pilgrimage.

It can be said that the conclusion and seal of the Seder night with the paragraph "Next year in Jerusalem" is not only a prayer and blessing for the future redemption, but also the summary of "tonight," which relates also to the *avodah* in this time that brings the future redemption—an *avodah* connected with the standing and situation of "**Jerusalem**" specifically.

**I. Regarding "Jerusalem," we find (in connection to our subject) two matters:** (1) "One does not leave the dead overnight in it," (2) "A person never said to his fellow, 'The place is too cramped for me to sleep in Jerusalem.'"

Their meaning in *avodas ha'adam*:

Death, the opposite of life, points to a standing and situation that is the opposite of *kedushah*, for *kedushah* is life. Therefore, "one does not leave the dead overnight in it," for in Jerusalem it is impossible for a matter that is the opposite of *kedushah* (death) to exist in a permanent manner ("leaving overnight").

In addition to the negation of the opposite of *kedushah*, Jerusalem also emphasizes the perfection of *kedushah* in a manner that is above measurement and limitation—"A person never said to his fellow, 'The place is too cramped for me to sleep in Jerusalem,'" for Jerusalem is above the limitation of place.

According to this, it is understood that the conclusion and seal of the Seder with the matter of Jerusalem is the summary of the entire *avodah* of "tonight"—the ultimate negation of all undesirable matters of sin and death ("one does not leave the dead overnight in it"), through which one leaves the exile and comes to the future redemption in a manner that is above the limitations of time and place ("A person never said to his fellow, 'The place is too cramped for me to sleep in Jerusalem'").

**J. It should be added in the explanation of the phrase "Next year *habah* (coming) in Jerusalem":**

The word "habah" can be interpreted in two ways: (1) "*Habah*" with an emphasis on the letter *beis*—that it is coming now (in the present), or that it has already come (in the past); (2) "Ha*ba*ah" with an emphasis on the letter *alef*—that it will come in the future.

It is worth noting the saying of the Rebbe Rayatz that Jews in their prayers are not particular about the rules of grammar, *mil'eil* and *mil'ra*, and the like, and the *avodah* of the angels is to arrange the combinations of the letters precisely, etc.

According to this, the meaning of "Next year *habah* in Jerusalem" can be interpreted in both ways simultaneously: "Next year *habah* (in the present and past) in Jerusalem"—that on this night we are already in the standing and situation of "Jerusalem," above any connection to sin, etc. And this is the empowerment that the future redemption will come—"Next year *haba'ah* (in the future) in Jerusalem."

(And the Rebbe concluded:)

"Next year in Jerusalem"—**through Mashiach Tzidkeinu.**

## Sicha of the Second Night of Pesach, 5712

**A. It is stated in the writings of the Arizal that the recitation of the Haggadah (on both nights) must be done "in a loud voice and with great joy." In general, there must be joy on the night of Pesach, for "there is no time of joy—the joy of the Shechinah—like the night of Pesach".**

**Therefore, I was puzzled by the fact that with the Rebbe, my father-in-law, one did not see joy on the night of Pesach; on the contrary, he experienced a state of tears. Although there are also tears of joy, in this specific instance, it was not evident that the tears were of joy.**

**In any case, since it is written in the writings of the Arizal that the night of Pesach must be with joy, we shall now play a *nigun*. And since among the nigunim themselves there are nigunim of bitterness and nigunim of joy, we shall play a nigun of *joy*, and play it *with joy*, and intend that this nigun of joy is connected to the order and recitation of the Haggadah, which, according to the words of the Arizal, must be done with joy.**

**[The Rebbe instructed that a joyful nigun be played.]**

**B. "*Ha Lachma Anya, the bread of affliction that our fathers ate in the land of Egypt*":**

**There is a *maamar* titled *Ha Lachma Anya* (found in a notebook of Chassidus that arrived from Eretz Yisrael, where most of the maamarim are by the Tzemach Tzedek, and from the style of the language it appears that this maamar is also by the Tzemach Tzedek) which asks: Why do we say of the matzah we are eating, "the bread of affliction that our fathers ate *in the land of Egypt*," even though the matzah we are eating corresponds to the matzah that Bnei Yisrael ate *when they left Egypt*?**

**It is known that there are two types of matzah: (1) Matzah from before midnight—as it is written, "In the evening you shall eat matzos," whose consumption is together with the Korban Pesach ("And they shall eat the meat... with matzos and maror"), the time for which is until midnight. (2) Matzah from after midnight—as it is written, "And they baked the dough which they brought out of Egypt into cakes of matzah... for they were driven out of Egypt and could not delay," which occurred after midnight.**

**Regarding this, we say in the Haggadah: "This matzah that we are eating... is because the dough of our fathers did not have time to become leavened before the King of the Kings of Kings, the Holy One, Blessed be He, revealed Himself to them and redeemed them, as it is said: 'And they baked the dough... cakes of matzah... for they were driven out of Egypt and could not delay.'" This means that the matzah *we* eat *before midnight* (at the time of eating the Korban Pesach) corresponds to the matzah *from after midnight*, when the dough did not have time to become leavened before the King of the Kings of Kings, the Holy One, Blessed be He, revealed Himself to them and redeemed them.**

**Since the matzah we eat corresponds to the matzah from after midnight, whose baking and eating took place *when they left Egypt* ("And they baked... for they were *driven out* of Egypt"), why do we say, "the bread of affliction that our fathers ate *in the land of Egypt*"?**

**C. The core explanation for this:**

**"Lachma Anya" ("bread of affliction") is matzah kneaded with water alone, which has no taste, unlike "matzah ashira" (rich matzah), which is kneaded with fruit juice, wine, oil, milk, and honey, and possesses taste.**

**Their significance in the *avodah* of man: *Matzah ashira* points to *havana and hassaga* (intellectual comprehension and grasp—"taste"), as hinted at by its kneading with wine, oil, milk, and honey; wine and oil represent Chochma and Binah, and milk and honey represent Da'as, which divides into Chesed and Gevurah. *Lachma Anya* points to *kabbalas ol* (acceptance of the yoke), for it lacks the *geshmak* (taste) of intellectual comprehension; rather, his *avodah* is from the perspective of *kabbalas ol*, as hinted at by its kneading with water, which has no taste (which is why one does not recite a blessing over water unless he is drinking it to quench his thirst).**

**Since in Egypt they were at the lowest possible level, even when the King of the Kings of Kings, the Holy One, Blessed be He, revealed Himself to them, the revelation was not in an internal manner, but in a *makif* (encompassing) manner. It was not drawn down into their *havana and hassaga*, but only in a manner of *kabbalas ol* alone. Therefore, even the matzah from after midnight (when the King of the Kings of Kings, the Holy One, Blessed be He, revealed Himself to them) is "bread of affliction," because *from their own perspective* (without the revelation from above), they were in the status and situation *of Egypt*.**

**And it is similar every single year: the revelation of the King of the Kings of Kings, the Holy One, Blessed be He, on the night of Pesach is in a *makif* manner (and its drawing down into the internal is through the *avodah* of Sefiras HaOmer), which is expressed (not in *havana and hassaga*, but) specifically in *kabbalas ol*. Therefore, the matzah we eat, which corresponds to the matzah from after midnight, is also "bread of affliction," because it is from the perspective of the revelation from above (the King of the Kings of Kings, the Holy One, Blessed be He, revealed Himself to them), and not from the perspective of man's *avodah*, for from his own perspective, he is in the status and situation of *Mitzrayim* (limitations)—"the *bread of affliction* that our fathers ate *in the land of Egypt*."**

**D. "*The son asks*":**

**Our custom is that the one asking prefaces his recitation of *Mah Nishtanah* with: "Tatte, I will ask you four questions."
